17. Transfer Pricing

Where it fits: Sections 92 to 94B of the Income-tax Act, 1961 govern computation of income from cross-border (and specified domestic) transactions between related parties at Arm's Length Price (ALP), so that profits are not shifted out of/into India through non-market pricing. This is the core "TP" chapter tested with both theory and numericals (ALP methods, range concept, secondary adjustment).

Sec. 92 — Charging Section

  • Key rule(s)/formula(s):
  • Any income, expenditure, interest, or allocation/apportionment of cost or expense arising from an international transaction (IT) or a specified domestic transaction (SDT) shall be computed having regard to the Arm's Length Price (ALP).
  • ALP = the price that would be charged/paid in a comparable transaction between unrelated persons in uncontrolled conditions.
  • TP provisions do not apply if the ALP computation would reduce income or increase the loss of the assessee (i.e., TP is a one-way, revenue-protective mechanism).
  • Concept: This is the anchor charging provision — every other TP section only tells you how to determine or enforce the ALP that Sec. 92 mandates.
  • Pitfalls / exam tips:
  • Students often forget the "no downward adjustment" rule — always test whether the ALP adjustment increases or decreases income before applying it.

Sec. 92B — International Transaction (IT)

  • Key rule(s):
  • IT = a transaction between two or more Associated Enterprises (AEs), where at least one AE is a Non-Resident (NR).
  • Covers: purchase/sale/lease of tangible or intangible property, provision of services, lending/borrowing of money, cost allocation/contribution arrangements, business restructuring, etc.
  • If both AEs are NR, TP applies only if the income of at least one of them is chargeable to tax under the Act (i.e., has an India nexus).
  • Deemed international transaction: A transaction between the assessee and an unrelated (third) person is deemed an IT if, in substance, it is influenced by an AE — i.e. either:
  • (a) there exists a prior agreement between the AE and the unrelated person, or
  • (b) the terms of the transaction are in substance determined by the AE.
  • Concept: Extends TP to "structured" arrangements that route real economic influence of an AE through a nominally unrelated counterparty.
  • Pitfalls / exam tips:
  • In deeming-fiction questions, identify who actually "determines the terms" — that is the trigger, not mere common ownership.

Sec. 92A — Associated Enterprise (AE)

  • Key rule(s)/thresholds: Two enterprises are AEs if, at any time during the P.Y., one of the following (direct or indirect) conditions is satisfied:
ClauseTestThreshold
(a)/(b)Shareholding (voting power), directly or via a common holder≥ 26%
(c)/(d)Appointment of Board of Directors / Executive Director(s), directly or via a common appointerMore than half the BOD, or one or more Executive Directors
(e)Loan by one enterprise to the other≥ 51% of book value of total assets of the borrower
(f)Guarantee of borrowings≥ 10% of total borrowings guaranteed
(g)Dependence on know-how/patent/copyright etc.Wholly dependent
(h)Supply of raw material, price/terms influenced≥ 90% of raw material supplied by the other, with influenced pricing
(i)Sale of manufactured goods, price/terms influenced by buyerSale is influenced (no numeric threshold)
(j)Common control by an individual/relatives—
(k)Common control by an HUF/its members/relatives—
(l)Interest in a firm/AOP/BOI≥ 10% interest
(m)Any other prescribed relationship of mutual interestAs prescribed
  • Concept: AE status is the gateway condition for Sec. 92B — without an AE relationship there is no "international transaction" to begin with.
  • Pitfalls / exam tips:
  • Both direct and indirect holding/control count (chain relationships, e.g., a parent's indirect grip on a step-down subsidiary via an intermediate AE).
  • Learn the percentages precisely — 26% (shareholding/interest test differs at 10% for firm/AOP/BOI), 51% (loan/assets), 10% (guarantee), 90% (raw material) are frequently tested individually.

Sec. 92C — Computation of ALP: The Five Methods

  • Key formula(s):

1. Comparable Uncontrolled Price Method (CUP)

ALP = Price in comparable uncontrolled transaction ± Adjustment for differences

2. Resale Price Method (RPM)

ALP = Resale price to unrelated party - Normal gross profit margin - Purchase-related expenses

3. Cost Plus Method (CPM)

ALP = Direct cost + Indirect cost of production + Normal gross profit margin ± Adjustment

4. Profit Split Method (PSM)

ALP (for an AE) = Cost incurred by that AE + Its share of combined profit (as per FAR-based ratio)
where combined profit = total consideration received in the joint venture − total cost incurred, split among AEs by relative contribution (functions performed, assets employed, risks assumed — FAR analysis).

5. Transactional Net Margin Method (TNMM)

  • Net profit margin (e.g., OP/OC, OP/Sales) earned by the assessee in the controlled transaction is compared with the net margin earned by comparable independent enterprises in similar transactions.
  • Concept / when to use each:
  • CUP — when a directly comparable uncontrolled price is available (most reliable if it exists).
  • RPM — when goods purchased from an AE are resold to unrelated parties without value addition (typical distributor).
  • CPM — when goods/services are manufactured/provided by one AE and supplied to another AE (typical contract manufacturer).
  • PSM — for integrated/joint-venture transactions where unique intangibles of both AEs contribute to profits (e.g., joint software development).
  • TNMM — most commonly used residual method where net margins (not gross margins/prices) are the most reliable comparability point.
  • Sec. 92C(2) — Tolerance Band (variation allowed between ALP and transaction price):
  • If more than one price results from application of the most appropriate method, ALP = arithmetic mean of such prices (applicable only when the dataset has fewer than 6 comparable values — see Rule 10CA below for 6 or more).
  • If the difference between actual transaction price and ALP is within 3% of the actual transaction price (1% for "wholesale trading"), the actual price is deemed to be the ALP (variation ignored).
  • If the difference exceeds the tolerance band, the entire difference is added to income (not just the excess over the band).
  • "Wholesale trading" (for the 1% band) means trading in goods where both:
  • (i) purchase cost of finished goods is ≥ 80% of total cost of the trading activity, and
  • (ii) average monthly closing stock is ≤ 10% of sales of the trading activity.
  • Sec. 92C(3) — ALP determined by the Assessing Officer (AO):
  • AO may determine ALP himself if: (i) assessee fails to compute ALP per the prescribed method; (ii) data used is unreliable/incorrect; (iii) documents under Sec. 92D not maintained; or (iv) info/documents required u/s 92D not furnished — subject to an opportunity of being heard.
  • Sec. 92C(4) — Effect of AO's ALP determination:
  • The other AE's income is not correspondingly adjusted (no automatic corresponding relief) — i.e., a one-sided (asymmetric) increase.
  • Chapter VI-A deduction and Sec. 10AA deduction are not allowed on the enhanced portion of income arising from the ALP adjustment.
  • Pitfalls / exam tips:
  • Tolerance band is 1% only for wholesale trading, 3% for everything else — a very frequently tested distinction.
  • Remember: if variation crosses the band, the whole difference (not merely excess over 3%/1%) is added — a common numerical trap.
  • PSM numericals: always first compute total JV profit, split by the FAR ratio, then add the AE's own cost to get its ALP.

Rule 10CA — Range Concept (6 or more comparables)

  • Key rule(s):
  • Applies only when the comparable dataset has 6 or more values; if fewer than 6, arithmetic mean under Sec. 92C(2) applies instead.
  • Applicable to CUP, RPM, CPM, and TNMM (not PSM).
  • Steps:
  1. Arrange the dataset in ascending order.
  2. Determine the range from the 35th percentile to the 65th percentile.
  3. If the actual transaction price falls within this range, it is deemed the ALP.
  4. If it falls outside the range, the median (50th percentile) of the dataset is deemed the ALP.
  • Percentile position formula: Position = n × percentile100 where n = number of values in the dataset.
  • If the position is not a whole number, round up to the next whole number and take that ranked value.
  • If the position is a whole number, take the average of the value at that rank and the next rank.
  • Multiple-year data: if data for the current year plus the preceding two years is used, take the weighted average — weighted by Sales (RPM), Cost incurred (CPM), or Cost/Sales/Assets employed (TNMM).
  • Concept: A statistical alternative to the simple arithmetic mean, meant to reflect a realistic dispersion of comparables rather than a single-point average.
  • Pitfalls / exam tips:
  • Don't confuse "not a whole number → round up" with "whole number → average of that rank and the next" — these are opposite rules and are commonly swapped by mistake.
  • Always sort ascending first; the percentile position formula is on the count of the full dataset, not on the transaction price.

Sec. 92CA — Reference to Transfer Pricing Officer (TPO)

  • Key rule(s):
  • AO may refer ALP computation to the TPO with previous approval of CIT/PCIT, if considered necessary/expedient.
  • TPO gathers material, may require the assessee to furnish evidence, and can also take up any other IT/SDT that comes to its notice during the reference (even if not originally referred by the AO).
  • TPO's order is binding on the AO.
  • TPO must pass the order at least 60 days prior to the time limit for completing assessment u/s 153; if the residual time (after excluding stay periods / time for information u/s 90/90A) is less than 60 days, it is extended to 60 days.
  • If a reference is made to the TPO, the time limit u/s 153 for the AO is extended by 1 year.
  • TPO must be a Joint/Deputy/Assistant Commissioner authorised by the CBDT.
  • ALP carried forward: once TPO determines ALP for a year, the assessee may opt for the same ALP to apply for the next 2 years if: the assessee opts for both years in the prescribed form/manner/time, and the TPO confirms validity in writing within 1 month from the end of the month of exercise of option (not available in search cases).
  • Concept: The TPO is the specialised authority who actually benchmarks/tests the transaction; the AO merely refers and gives effect.
  • Pitfalls / exam tips:
  • Remember the 60-day buffer before Sec. 153 time-bar and the 1-year extension to AO's time limit — both are separately tested.

Sec. 92CB — Safe Harbour Rules (SHR)

  • Key rule(s):
  • CBDT may notify Safe Harbour Rules for determination of ALP or income u/s 9(1)(i).
  • "Safe harbour" = circumstances in which the income-tax authorities shall accept the transfer price declared by the assessee (no further TP scrutiny on that transaction if conditions are met).
  • Concept: A voluntary, low-litigation compliance option — assessee accepts a pre-notified minimum margin/profit level in exchange for certainty and no TPO adjustment.
  • Pitfalls / exam tips:
  • Detailed sector-wise safe harbour margins (IT/ITeS, KPO, contract R&D, auto loans, etc.) are periodically updated by CBDT notification — treat specific percentage margins as subject to the latest notification in force for the relevant AY rather than memorising a single fixed figure.

Sec. 92CC & 92CD — Advance Pricing Agreement (APA)

  • Key rule(s):
  • CBDT, with approval of Central Government, may enter into an APA with any person to determine: (a) the ALP / manner of determining ALP for a future IT, or (b) income attributable to a NR's PE operations in India u/s 9(1)(i).
  • Maximum period: APA is valid for up to 5 consecutive years.
  • Roll-back: APA may also apply to up to 4 years preceding the first year covered by the APA, subject to conditions (same IT, ROI filed within Sec. 139(1) due date, Form 3CEB report furnished u/s 92E, roll-back requested for all years in which the IT occurred).
  • Binding on: (a) the applicant, and (b) the Income-tax authorities up to the level of CIT/PCIT — not binding if there is a change in law or facts.
  • CBDT (with CG approval) may declare an APA void ab initio if obtained by fraud/misrepresentation — the Act then applies as if the APA never existed; the intervening period is excluded from any statutory time limit (e.g. Sec. 153), and if the residual time after exclusion is less than 60 days, it is extended to 60 days.
  • Once an APA application is filed, proceedings for that person are deemed pending.
  • If Sec. 92CC applies, Sec. 92C and 92CA do not apply (APA overrides the normal ALP-determination machinery).
  • Rollback not available where: (i) the ALP for that year was already the subject matter of an ITAT appeal disposed of before the APA is signed; or (ii) applying rollback would reduce total income/increase loss versus the returned income/loss (per Rule 10MA).
  • Application fees (based on value of IT):
Value of International TransactionAPA Application Fee
Up to ₹100 crore₹10 lakh
> ₹100 crore up to ₹200 crore₹15 lakh
> ₹200 crore₹20 lakh
  • Roll-back application: Form 3CEDA, fee ₹5,00,000.
  • Withdrawal of application permitted any time before finalisation, but the fee is not refundable.
  • Annual compliance report to be filed in quadruplicate, within 30 days of the ROI due date or 90 days of entering the APA, whichever is later; TPO's compliance audit report due within 6 months from the end of the month of receipt of the compliance report.
  • Sec. 92CD — Effect of APA on already-filed returns:
  • If a ROI was already furnished before entering the APA, a modified return must be filed within 3 months from the end of the month in which the APA was entered into.
  • If assessment is pending on the date of the modified return, AO completes assessment per the APA (AO gets 1 extra year).
  • If assessment is already completed, AO must modify total income within 1 year from the end of the FY in which the modified return was furnished.
  • Definitions:
TermMeaning
APAAgreement between CBDT (with CG approval) and taxpayer fixing the ALP/method in advance for future international transactions
Roll-back provisionExtension of the agreed ALP/method to up to 4 preceding years, subject to conditions
  • Concept: APA gives upfront certainty on transfer pricing outcomes for future years (and optionally past years via roll-back), reducing litigation.
  • Pitfalls / exam tips:
  • Don't confuse the 5-year forward period with the 4-year roll-back period — they are different caps and often tested together in one MCQ/case.
  • CBDT Circular 10/2015 clarifications: roll-back is "all 4 years or none" (not selective); available even if original return was later revised u/s 139(5), as long as the original return met the Sec. 139(1) due date; on amalgamation, only the surviving/successor company can claim roll-back for its own transactions, not the amalgamating company's.

Sec. 92CE — Secondary Adjustment

  • Key rule(s)/formula(s):
  • Primary adjustment = increase in income / reduction in loss due to an ALP adjustment.
  • Excess money = ALP (as per primary adjustment) − Actual transaction price.

Excess Money = ALP - Actual Transaction Price

  • Secondary adjustment: if the excess money held by the AE is not repatriated to India within the prescribed time, it is deemed to be an advance (loan) made by the assessee to the AE, and interest on such deemed loan is added to the assessee's income until repatriation (India follows the deemed loan approach, not deemed dividend).
  • Secondary adjustment applies where primary adjustment arises from: (i) suo-moto adjustment by assessee in ROI; (ii) AO's adjustment accepted by assessee; (iii) an APA entered on/after 1.4.2017; (iv) Safe Harbour Rules u/s 92CB; or (v) resolution under MAP (Mutual Agreement Procedure) u/s 90/90A.
  • De-minimis exemption: secondary adjustment not required if primary adjustment in a PY does not exceed ₹1 crore, or if it relates to AY 2016-17 or an earlier year.
  • Time limit for repatriation: 90 days (from the trigger date below); interest runs on unrepatriated excess money from the corresponding start date:
Primary adjustment made byRepatriation window startsInterest computed from
Order of AO/appellate authorityDate of the orderDate of the order
APA entered before ROI due dateActual date of filing ROIDue date of ROI
APA entered after ROI due dateEnd of month APA enteredEnd of month APA entered
Mutual Agreement Procedure (MAP)Date AO gives effect to MAPDate AO gives effect to MAP
Other reasonsDue date of ROIDue date of ROI
  • Rate of interest on excess money (Rule 10CB):

Rate (INR-denominated IT) = SBI 1-year MCLR (as on 1st April of the P.Y.) + 3.25%
Rate (Foreign-currency-denominated IT) = 6-month LIBOR (as on 30th Sept. of the P.Y.) + 3%

  • ⚠️ VERIFY — the foreign-currency benchmark is prescribed in Rule 10CB as "6-month LIBOR"; since LIBOR has been globally discontinued (USD LIBOR ceased mid-2023), confirm whether CBDT has since notified a replacement reference rate (e.g., a SOFR/ARR-based rate) for the relevant assessment year before relying on this in a foreign-currency numerical.
  • Sec. 92CE(2A) — Option to pay Additional Income-tax instead of Secondary Adjustment:
  • Assessee may instead pay a one-time additional income-tax at an effective rate of 20.9664% [18% base rate + 12% surcharge + 4% health & education cess] on the excess money not repatriated, in addition to interest already accrued till the date of such payment.

Additional Tax = Excess Money (unrepatriated) × 20.9664%

  • This tax is a final payment — no credit is allowed, and no deduction is allowed for the amount on which such tax is paid under any other provision.
  • Once this additional tax is paid, no further secondary adjustment/interest accrues from the date of payment.
  • Concept: Secondary adjustment ensures that even after the primary income addition, any cash benefit still parked abroad with the AE is taxed as if it were a loan (interest income) — or the assessee can "buy out" this exposure via a one-time tax.
  • Pitfalls / exam tips:
  • The ₹1 crore de-minimis threshold and the 90-day repatriation window are the two most tested numbers here.
  • In interest computation, always identify the correct "start date" from the table above — it varies by the trigger of the primary adjustment.

Sec. 92D & Sec. 92E — Documentation and Audit Report

  • Key rule(s)/thresholds:
  • Sec. 92D: Every person entering into an IT/SDT must maintain prescribed documents (TP study report / Local File); a constituent entity of an international group must also maintain Master File information; AO/CIT(A) may call for the TP study report — to be furnished within 10 days of notice (extendable by a further 30 days).
  • Documentation required only if the value of the IT exceeds ₹1 crore.
  • Documents must be retained for 8 years from the end of the relevant AY.
  • Sec. 92E: Every person entering into an IT/SDT must obtain and furnish an accountant's report in Form 3CEB, due on or before 31st October of the AY (one month before the ROI due date applicable to such assessees).
  • Penalties:
SectionDefaultPenalty
271AAFailure to maintain/report TP documents, Local File, or Master File info2% of the value of the transaction
271GFailure to furnish information/documents to AO/TPO2% of the value of the transaction
271BAFailure to furnish the CA's report (Form 3CEB) u/s 92E₹1,00,000
270AFailure to report a transaction — treated as "misreporting of income"200% of tax on the under-reported income
  • Note: Under-reported income (u/s 270A) does not include the amount of any addition made strictly in conformity with the ALP determined by the TPO, provided the assessee had maintained documentation, disclosed the transaction, and revealed all material facts.
  • Concept: Documentation (92D) is the evidentiary backbone; the CA's Form 3CEB report (92E) is the independent certification filed with the return.
  • Pitfalls / exam tips:
  • ₹1 crore is the documentation threshold under Sec. 92D — don't confuse with the ₹20 crore SDT aggregation threshold (Sec. 92BA) or the ₹1 crore secondary-adjustment de-minimis (Sec. 92CE).

Sec. 92BA — Specified Domestic Transactions (SDT)

  • Key rule(s):
  • TP provisions extend to specified domestic (non-cross-border) related-party transactions if the aggregate value in the P.Y. exceeds ₹20 crore.
  • Covered transactions include: inter-unit transfer of goods/services referred to in Sec. 80A & Sec. 80-IA(8); transactions with "closely connected persons" u/s 80-IA(10); any other Chapter VI-A/Sec. 10AA transaction to which Sec. 80-IA(8)/(10) applies; transactions with closely connected persons u/s 115BAB(6)/115BAE(4); and any other prescribed transaction.
  • Concept: SDT provisions target profit-shifting between units of the same domestic entity/group (e.g., an eligible unit claiming a tax holiday transacting at non-market prices with a non-eligible unit) — same ALP machinery as international transactions applies, but the ₹1%/3% tolerance band relief does still apply (only the NJA fiction u/s 94A denies it).
  • Pitfalls / exam tips:
  • The SDT aggregation threshold (₹20 crore) was raised from the earlier ₹5 crore — remember the current figure of ₹20 crore.

Sec. 286 & Rule 10DA — Country-by-Country Report (CbCR) and Master File

  • Key rule(s)/thresholds:
  • CbCR (Sec. 286): parent entity (or alternate reporting entity) resident in India of an international group must file the CbC report in Form 3CEAD with the JDIT (as designated by DGIT/PDGIT-Systems) within 12 months from the end of the relevant accounting year, if consolidated group revenue (per CFS) of the preceding year is ₹6,400 crore or more.
  • Exchange rate for converting foreign-currency revenue to INR: TT buying rate (TTBR) on the last day of the accounting year.
  • Every resident constituent entity of a group whose parent is a NR must notify (Form 3CEAC) at least 2 months prior to the CbCR due date, stating whether it is the alternate reporting entity and details of the parent/ARE.
  • A resident constituent entity (other than the parent/ARE) must itself file the CbCR in India if the foreign parent's jurisdiction: does not require CbCR filing, has no exchange arrangement with India, or has a "systemic failure" of exchange (notified by JDIT) — in the systemic-failure case, filing is due within 6 months from the end of the month of such intimation.
  • If multiple resident constituent entities exist, the group may nominate one via Form 3CEAE to file on the group's behalf.
  • JDIT may call for verification documents — to be furnished within 30 days (extendable by a further 30 days).
  • Penalties (Sec. 271GB):
DefaultPenalty
Failure to furnish CbCR — up to 1 month delay₹5,000 per day
Failure to furnish CbCR — beyond 1 month₹15,000 per day (after the first month)
Continuing default after penalty order served₹50,000 per day from date of service
Failure to furnish information/documents on verification₹5,000/day after the allowed period; ₹50,000/day if continuing after penalty order
Inaccurate information furnished in the CbC report (knowingly, or not corrected within 15 days of discovery, or in response to notice)₹5,00,000
  • Sec. 273B: no Sec. 271GB penalty if there was reasonable cause for the failure.
  • Master File (Rule 10DA): A constituent entity must maintain and furnish the Master File (Form 3CEAA) if:
  • (i) consolidated group revenue (per CFS) for the accounting year exceeds ₹500 crore, and
  • (ii) aggregate value of international transactions (as per books) exceeds ₹50 crore, or in respect of purchase/sale/transfer/lease/use of intangible property exceeds ₹10 crore.
  • Part A of Form 3CEAA must be filed by every constituent entity every year regardless of the above limits; Part B only if the limits are breached.
  • Due date: on or before the due date for furnishing the ROI.
  • If multiple resident constituent entities exist, one entity may file on the group's behalf if designated, and Form 3CEAB is furnished to the JDIT at least 30 days before the Form 3CEAA due date.
  • Documents to be retained for 8 years from the end of the relevant AY.
  • Definitions:
TermMeaning
Alternate Reporting Entity (ARE)Entity designated by the group to file CbCR instead of the parent entity
Accounting yearThe P.Y. (1 April–31 March) if parent is resident in India; otherwise the accounting period the parent uses under its own country's law/AS
  • Concept: CbCR gives tax authorities a jurisdiction-wise picture (revenue, profit, tax paid, employees, capital, assets) of a large MNE group; the Master File gives a group-wide organisational/functional overview — both are BEPS Action 13 outputs.
  • Pitfalls / exam tips:
  • Keep the three monetary thresholds distinct: ₹6,400 crore (CbCR applicability), ₹500 crore (Master File — group revenue), ₹50 crore / ₹10 crore (Master File — IT value / intangible IT value).

Sec. 94A — Transactions with Persons Located in a Notified Jurisdictional Area (NJA)

  • Key rule(s):
  • Central Government may notify any country/territory as a Notified Jurisdictional Area (NJA) (non-cooperative jurisdiction).
  • If the assessee transacts with a person located in an NJA:
  • (i) all parties to the transaction are deemed AEs (Sec. 92A fiction);
  • (ii) the transaction is deemed an international transaction (Sec. 92B fiction);
  • (iii) TP provisions apply, except the tolerance band relief under Sec. 92C(2) (i.e., no 1%/3% cushion);
  • (iv) assessee must furnish documents/information as required by the tax authority;
  • (v) minimum TDS rate of 30% applies on payments to NJA persons;
  • (vi) any sum received from an NJA person must be explained by the assessee (source of funds in the hands of that person, or the beneficial owner if different) — failing satisfactory explanation, the sum is treated as income of the assessee.
  • Concept: A deterrent/anti-avoidance provision targeting transactions routed through jurisdictions that do not cooperate on information exchange with India.
  • Pitfalls / exam tips:
  • No country has, as of now, actually been notified as an NJA under this section — it is tested purely as a conditional/contingent provision (frame answers as "if a country is notified…").

Sec. 94B — Limitation on Interest Deduction (Thin Capitalisation)

  • Key formula(s)/rule(s):
  • Applies to interest (or similar charges like guarantee commission) paid/payable by an Indian company or a PE of a foreign company in India on debt issued by a non-resident AE — disallows the "Excess Interest":

Excess Interest = min[ Total interest to NR AE - 30% of EBITDA , Total interest paid/payable to the AE ]
(EBITDA = Earnings Before Interest, Tax, Depreciation and Amortisation)

  • Applies only if interest paid/payable to the AE exceeds ₹1 crore in the P.Y.
  • Implicit/explicit guarantee or back-to-back deposit fiction: if a loan is from a non-AE lender, but an AE provides an implicit/explicit guarantee, or deposits a matching amount with that lender, the debt is deemed to be issued by an AE.
  • Carry forward: disallowed excess interest can be carried forward and deducted (against PGBP income of the same nature, within the Sec. 94B limit) for a maximum of 8 assessment years immediately succeeding the year of disallowance.
  • Not applicable to: an Indian company/PE of a foreign company engaged in the banking or insurance business, a finance company located in an IFSC (per Notification No. 10/2025 — see below), or interest on debt issued by a lender that is a PE in India of a NR bank or a notified class of NBFC.
  • ⚠️ VERIFY — Notification No. 10/2025 (excluding IFSC finance companies from Sec. 94B) is a recent notification; confirm its exact number/date and conditions (no public deposits accepted, not registered as a Banking Unit, restricted to permissible IFSC treasury/lending/factoring activities, and borrowing in foreign currency) against the CBDT notification in force for the AY being examined.
  • Concept: Implements BEPS Action 4 — caps interest-expense erosion of the Indian tax base through excessive related-party debt (thin capitalisation).
  • Pitfalls / exam tips:
  • "Total interest" for the 30%-of-EBITDA limb is generally taken as interest paid to the NR AE only (per the Finance Bill memorandum) — this is a commonly tested nuance versus "total interest expense of the company."
  • The ₹1 crore threshold is a de-minimis gate, not a slab — below it, Sec. 94B does not apply at all.

References

  1. Income-tax Act, 1961 — Sections 92, 92A, 92B, 92BA, 92C, 92CA, 92CB, 92CC, 92CD, 92CE, 92D, 92E, 94A, 94B, 271AA, 271BA, 271G, 271GB, 273B, 286 (as amended up to Finance Act, 2025), for AE thresholds, ALP methods, tolerance band, secondary adjustment, thin-capitalisation and CbCR/Master File framework.
  2. Income-tax Rules, 1962 — Rule 10CA (Range concept), Rule 10CB (secondary adjustment interest rate), Rule 10DA (Master File), Rule 10G/10MA (APA fees/roll-back conditions), for the specific percentiles, benchmark interest rates, and monetary thresholds.
  3. CBDT Circular No. 10/2015 — clarifications on APA roll-back provisions (selectivity, revised returns, amalgamation/demerger effect).
  4. ICAI — Final Course Study Material, Direct Tax Laws & International Taxation (Transfer Pricing chapter), for method definitions and worked illustrations consistent with current syllabus treatment.

18. Non-Resident & NRI Taxation

Where it fits: Before computing tax you must fix residential status (Sec. 6) because it decides the scope of total income (Sec. 5); Sec. 7/9 then pull specific foreign-flavoured incomes into the Indian tax net, Chapter XII-A/Sec.115A-series fix special flat rates for NR/NRI income, and this chapter is the gateway to Ch. 19 (DTAA relief).

Residential Status of Individuals [Sec. 6(1), 6(1A), 6(6)]

  • Key formula(s)/rule(s):

Resident (Sec. 6(1)) ⇔ Stay in India ≥ 182 days in P.Y. OR (Stay ≥ 60 days in P.Y. AND Stay ≥ 365 days in preceding 4 P.Y.'s)

ROR (Sec. 6(6)) ⇔ Resident in ≥ 2 of preceding 10 P.Y.'s AND Stay ≥ 730 days in preceding 7 P.Y.'s (else R but NOR)

  • Satisfying any one basic condition ⇒ Resident; satisfying neither ⇒ Non-Resident.
  • Special relaxation (2nd basic condition ignored, only 182-day test applies) for: (a) Indian citizen leaving India for employment abroad; (b) Indian citizen who is a crew member of an Indian ship leaving India; (c) Indian citizen/Person of Indian Origin (PIO) "visiting" India whose total income (excluding foreign-source income) is up to ₹15 lakh in the P.Y.
  • 120-day rule: Indian citizen/PIO visiting India whose total income (other than foreign-source income) exceeds ₹15 lakh — the 60-day limb is replaced by 120 days (instead of 182). If he stays 120 days or more but less than 182 days, and 365 days or more in the last 4 P.Y.'s, he is directly R but NOR (additional conditions of 6(6) need not be checked).
  • Day of entry into India and day of departure from India both count as stay in India.
  • Foreign-bound Indian ship crew: period from date entered in CDC (Continuous Discharge Certificate) on joining the ship to date entered in CDC on signing off is treated as stay outside India.
  • Concept: Residential status is redetermined every P.Y. independently for every assessee; it is the single biggest gate that decides how much of a person's global income India can tax.
  • Pitfalls / exam tips:
  • Don't apply the 120-day rule to someone who is not a "visiting" Indian citizen/PIO — it never applies to foreign nationals.
  • "Foreign source income" (FSI) = income accruing/arising outside India, except income from a business controlled from or a profession set up in India — a favourite definitional trap.
  • The ₹15 lakh threshold is computed excluding foreign-source income, not total global income.
  • Definitions:
TermMeaning
Person of Indian Origin (PIO)He, or either parent, or either grandparent, was born in undivided India
R but NORResident satisfying at most one of the two Sec. 6(6) additional conditions

Sec. 6(1A) – Deemed Resident (Indian Citizen)

  • Key formula(s)/rule(s):

Deemed Resident (always R but NOR) ⇔ Indian citizen, Total Income (excl. FSI) > ₹15 lakh in P.Y. AND not liable to tax in any other country/territory by reason of domicile, residence, or similar criterion

  • This deeming provision does not apply if the individual is already resident under Sec. 6(1) for that P.Y. — it is a residual "stateless person" anti-abuse rule.
  • "Liable to tax" includes a person subsequently exempted from tax liability under that foreign country's law.
  • Concept: Plugs the loophole of high-net-worth Indian citizens engineering "non-resident of anywhere" status purely to escape residence-based taxation everywhere.
  • Pitfalls / exam tips: Always test Sec. 6(1) first; only fall back to 6(1A) if the person is Non-Resident under 6(1). A 6(1A) deemed resident is always R but NOR, never R&OR.

Residential Status – HUF, Firm/AOP/BOI, and Companies (incl. POEM)

  • Key formula(s)/rule(s):
  • HUF/Firm/AOP/BOI/Local Authority/AJP [Sec. 6(2)]: Resident if control & management of affairs is situated wholly or partly in India; Non-Resident only if control & management is wholly outside India. (HUF is further ROR/RNOR based on whether its Karta satisfies both Sec. 6(6) additional conditions.)
  • Indian company: always resident, irrespective of POEM.
  • Foreign company: resident only if its Place of Effective Management (POEM) is in India in that year.

POEM in India (foreign co.) ⇔ Place where key management & commercial decisions necessary for the conduct of the business as a whole are, in substance, made

  • POEM determination applies only if turnover/gross receipts > ₹50 crore in the F.Y. [CBDT Circular 8/2017] — companies at/below ₹50 crore are outside POEM scrutiny altogether.
  • Active Business Outside India (ABOI) test — POEM is not in India if (i) company has ABOI, and (ii) majority of Board meetings are held outside India. ABOI is satisfied only if all of:

Passive income ≤ 50% of total income; Indian assets < 50% of total assets; Employees in/resident of India < 50%; Payroll cost of Indian employees < 50% of total payroll

  • Passive income = income from purchase/sale transactions with associated enterprises + royalty/dividend/capital gains/interest (other than of a banking company/PFI)/rental income.
  • ABOI averages are computed over the P.Y. and the preceding 2 years (or the company's actual existence period if shorter), based on the applicable foreign accounting year.
  • If Board is not actually exercising powers (holding co./another India-resident person calls the shots) ⇒ POEM deemed in India. Mere adoption of global group policies on payroll/HR/IT/supply chain/banking does not by itself defeat POEM outside India.
  • For companies failing ABOI, POEM is a two-stage test: (1) identify who actually makes key management/commercial decisions; (2) locate where those decisions are, in substance, made — substance over form; place of decision-making trumps place of implementation.
  • Concept: POEM replaced the old "control and management" test for foreign companies (w.e.f. AY 2017-18) to curb round-tripping via shell foreign holding companies while shielding genuine multinational operations via the ABOI safe harbour.
  • Pitfalls / exam tips:
  • Shareholders' decisions on matters reserved for shareholders (e.g. approving accounts) are not relevant for POEM.
  • GAAR can still be invoked despite BOD following "objective global group policies" if the arrangement is abusive/aggressive tax planning.
  • ABOI + majority-outside-India board meetings ⇒ skip the two-stage test entirely.
  • Definitions:
TermMeaning
POEMPlace where key management & commercial decisions for the business as a whole are, in substance, made
ABOIActive Business Outside India — the 4-limb passive-income/assets/employees/payroll test

Sec. 115JH – Transition Mechanism for a Foreign Company Becoming POEM-Resident

  • Key formula(s)/rule(s): [Notification 29/2018, applicable from 1.4.2017]
  • Opening WDV/b/f losses: taken from the foreign country's tax records if the company was assessed to tax there (adjusted for notional depreciation if not actually allowed there); else taken from its books of account under foreign law.
  • Losses/unabsorbed depreciation of the foreign company can be set off only against income that becomes taxable in India solely because of POEM residency, and can be carried forward only for the remaining statutory period.
  • If TDS provisions applicable to it as resident and as foreign company conflict, only the foreign company TDS provision applies.
  • Tax rate remains the foreign company rate (35%) even after it becomes POEM-resident.
  • Foreign-company-specific provisions continue to apply; NR-specific provisions stop applying; resident-specific provisions start applying.
  • Concept: A one-time bridging mechanism so a genuinely foreign company that accidentally trips POEM residency isn't whipsawed on opening balances, brought-forward losses, and applicable tax rate.
  • Pitfalls / exam tips: Remember the "≥6 months ⇒ separate accounting year, <6 months ⇒ merge with preceding year" stub-period rule when the foreign accounting year doesn't end on 31 March.

Sec. 5 – Scope of Total Income & Taxability Matrix

  • Key formula(s)/rule(s):

Total Income = Indian Income + (Foreign Income, only if assessee is Resident)

Indian Income = Income received/deemed received in India OR Income accruing/arising/deemed to accrue or arise in India

IncomeR & ORR but NORNR
Indian IncomeTaxableTaxableTaxable
Foreign Income — Business/Profession controlled/set up from IndiaTaxableTaxableNot Taxable
Foreign Income — OtherTaxableNot TaxableNot Taxable

For assessees other than Individual/HUF: Resident — Indian + Foreign Income taxable; NR — only Indian Income taxable.

  • Concept: This is the master switch — everything else in this chapter (Sec. 7, Sec. 9) exists only to define what counts as "Indian income."
  • Pitfalls / exam tips:
  • Income received in cash or in kind both count; "received" means received for the first time — a later remittance of already-received foreign income into India is not a fresh receipt.
  • Income already taxed on accrual basis is not taxed again on subsequent remittance, regardless of residential status.

Sec. 7 & Sec. 9(1)(i) – Deemed Receipt and Business Connection / SEP

  • Key formula(s)/rule(s):
  • Sec. 7 (deemed received in India): (i) employer's contribution to RPF in excess of 12% of salary, or interest credited in excess of 9.5% p.a.; (ii) employer's contribution under NPS (Sec. 80CCD); (iii) amount transferred from URPF to RPF (employer's contribution + interest thereon).
  • Sec. 9(1)(i): income through/from a business connection in India, or property/asset/source of income in India, or transfer of a capital asset situated in India, is deemed to accrue/arise in India — but only so much as is attributable to operations carried out in India [Explanation 3].
  • Business connection exists where a person acting on behalf of the NR habitually (a) has/exercises authority to conclude contracts on the NR's behalf (in the NR's name, or for transferring/granting use of NR-owned property, or for the NR's provision of services), or (b) maintains a stock and habitually delivers from it on the NR's behalf, or (c) habitually secures orders mainly/wholly for the NR. Independent agents (broker/general commission agent acting in the ordinary course of business, not working mainly/wholly for the NR) are excluded.
  • Significant Economic Presence (SEP) also constitutes business connection:

SEP (transactions) ⇔ Aggregate payments from transactions in goods/services/property (incl. data/software download) with any person in India > ₹2 crore in the P.Y.

SEP (digital interaction) ⇔ Systematic & continuous soliciting of business or interacting with users in India ≥ 3,00,000 users

SEP applies whether or not the agreement is signed in India, or the NR has a place of business/residence in India, or renders services in India. Only income attributable to the SEP transaction is taxed. Purchase of goods in India purely for export is excluded from SEP.

  • Explanation 3A (from AY 2021-22): income attributable to Indian operations includes income from (i) advertisement targeting Indian residents/IP addresses, (ii) sale of data collected from Indian residents/IP addresses, and (iii) sale of goods/services using such data.
  • Not business connection: purchase of goods in India for export; collection of news for transmission outside India; shooting of a cinematograph film in India by a non-citizen individual/firm with no Indian-citizen or India-resident partner; a foreign diamond-mining company's display of uncut/unassorted diamonds in a CG-notified special zone.
  • Concept: Sec. 9(1)(i) is the primary hook to tax a non-resident's India-sourced business income even without a fixed place of business — SEP extends this to the digital economy where physical presence is absent.
  • Pitfalls / exam tips: The ₹2 crore and 3,00,000-user SEP thresholds are prescribed by rule and are cumulative tests, not alternative floors for the same stream — test each limb (transaction value vs. user count) independently.

Sec. 9(1)(i) – Indirect Transfer of Shares (Explanation 5)

  • Key formula(s)/rule(s):

Shares of a foreign co./entity deemed situated in India ⇔ FMV of Indian assets > ₹10 crore AND FMV of Indian assets ≥ 50% of FMV of all assets, on the valuation date

Capital Gain taxable in India (Rule 11UC) = Total Capital Gain × FMV of Indian assets of the foreign companyFMV of total assets of the foreign company

  • Exception (no indirect-transfer taxation): transferor, together with associated enterprises, does not hold (a) the right of management/control, or (b) voting power/share capital/interest exceeding 5%, in the foreign entity at any time in the 12 months preceding the transfer.
  • Valuation date = last day of the accounting year preceding the transfer date; shifts to the actual date of transfer if the book value of assets on the transfer date exceeds the last balance-sheet book value by 15% or more.
  • FMV is computed without reducing liabilities. Where not all assets of the foreign entity are in India, only the proportionate income is deemed to accrue in India.
  • Assets held (directly/indirectly) by an NR through a Category-I Foreign Portfolio Investor are excluded from this deeming fiction even if value is substantially derived from Indian assets.
  • Concept: Explanation 5 was inserted to override the Supreme Court's Vodafone ruling and tax offshore share transfers that derive their value substantially from underlying Indian assets.
  • Pitfalls / exam tips: Always check the 15%-swing rule first to fix the correct valuation date before running the 10-crore/50% test — a common numeric trap in problems (see the Orange/Grapes/Mango worked illustration in source, where valuation date shifted back because BV had actually fallen, not risen 15%).
  • Definitions:
TermMeaning
Valuation dateNormally the last balance-sheet date before transfer; date of transfer itself if BV jumped ≥15%

Deemed Accrual – Salary, Dividend, Gift, Interest, Royalty, FTS [Sec. 9(1)(ii)–(viii)]

  • Key formula(s)/rule(s):
  • 9(1)(ii): Salary for services rendered in India is deemed to accrue in India (whether paid before/after rendering service — includes gratuity, pension, profit in lieu of salary).
  • 9(1)(iii): Salary paid by Government to an Indian citizen for services rendered outside India is deemed to accrue in India (perquisites/allowances exempt under Sec. 10(7)).
  • 9(1)(iv): Dividend paid by an Indian company, even outside India, is deemed to accrue in India.
  • 9(1)(viii) – Gift: money gifted by a resident to (a) a NR/foreign company on or after 5 July 2019, or (b) an R but NOR on or after 1 April 2023, is deemed to accrue/arise in India.
  • 9(1)(v) – Interest, deemed to accrue in India if payable by:

Interest deemed Indian-sourced ⇔ Payer is Govt. OR (textResident, except money borrowed & used for business/profession outside India or for earning income from a source outside India) OR (NR, if money borrowed is used for business/profession carried on in India)

Interest paid by an Indian PE of a NR bank to its Head Office/other PE outside India is always deemed to accrue in India.

  • 9(1)(vi) – Royalty and 9(1)(vii) – Fees for Technical Services (FTS) follow the identical payer-based structure as interest above (Government / Resident-with-outside-India-business exception / NR-using-in-India-business).
  • Royalty covers: transfer/licence of patent, invention, design, trademark, secret formula/process; imparting related information; use of such property; imparting technical/industrial/commercial/scientific knowledge/experience/skill; use of industrial/commercial/scientific equipment (excluding Sec. 44BB receipts); transfer of copyright/literary/artistic/scientific work (incl. films/video/audio tapes); and rendering of services connected with any of the above — including consideration for use of computer software.
  • FTS = consideration (incl. lump sum) for managerial, technical, or consultancy services (including supply of technical/other personnel), excluding construction/assembly/mining-type project consideration and anything taxable as "Salaries".
  • Lump-sum royalty paid by a resident to a NR for supply of computer software along with computer hardware, under a CG-approved scheme, is not deemed to accrue in India. If a transfer of property is already taxed as capital gains, it is not additionally treated as royalty.
  • Concept: These are the specific "source rule" deeming fictions that let India tax passive/technical payments made to non-residents even when the payer, not the NR, is physically in India.
  • Pitfalls / exam tips:
  • The "used for business/profession outside India" exception (for a resident payer) is the most tested carve-out — read it strictly against the purpose of borrowing/use, not merely where the payer is based.
  • Classic exam trap: NR-to-NR interest (e.g., "Lobo" borrows from "Hobo", both NR, invests in Indian shares) does not trigger Sec. 9(1)(v) even though the funds land in India.

Sec. 9A – Eligible Investment Fund (EIF) & Eligible Fund Manager

  • Key formula(s)/rule(s):
  • Fund management activity of an EIF carried out through an eligible fund manager (EFM) in India does not by itself create a business connection, nor make the EIF resident in India.
  • EIF key conditions (illustrative, not exhaustive): fund is NR; resident of a DTAA country; direct resident participation ≤ 5% of corpus (tested on 1 April & 1 October, curable within 4 months); ≥25 unrelated members; no member (with connected persons) holds >10% interest; top-10-members' aggregate interest <50%; investment in any one entity ≤20% of corpus; no investment in associate entities; monthly average corpus ≥ ₹100 crore; does not control/manage any business in India.
  • EFM conditions: not an employee/connected person of the fund; SEBI-registered fund manager/investment adviser; acting in the ordinary course of business; EFM (with connected persons) not entitled to more than 20% of the fund's profits from transactions it carries out.
  • Non-filing of the prescribed annual statement within 90 days from the end of the F.Y. attracts penalty of ₹5,00,000 under Sec. 271FAB.
  • Conditions (e), (f), (g) [90%-lending-style/associate/₹100 cr corpus tests] are relaxed for funds set up by a foreign Government/Central Bank/sovereign fund, or a Category-I/II FPI-linked fund.
  • CG may relax conditions for a fund/fund manager located in an IFSC that commenced operations on/before 31 March 2030.
  • Concept: A safe-harbour so genuine offshore funds don't become taxable in India merely because their portfolio manager sits in Mumbai/GIFT City.
  • Pitfalls / exam tips: This regime does not exempt income otherwise taxable regardless of the fund-manager's location — it only neutralises the "business connection via fund manager" risk.

Key Exemptions for Non-Residents & IFSC Units [Sec. 10(4), 10(4D)–10(4H), 10(6), 10(23FE), 10(23FF)]

  • Key formula(s)/rule(s): (selected high-yield items)
SectionExempt incomeEligible person
10(4)(ii)Interest on NRE account balancePerson resident outside India under FEMA / RBI-permitted account holder
10(4D)Capital gains / income from securities of a Specified FundAttributable to units held by NR (not a NR's Indian PE)
10(4E)Income from non-deliverable forward contracts, offshore derivative instruments, OTC derivatives with an IFSC OBU/FPINon-Resident
10(4F)Royalty/interest on lease of an aircraft/ship, paid by an IFSC unit that commenced ops on/before 31.3.2030Non-Resident
10(4H)Capital gains on equity shares of an IFSC aircraft/ship-leasing domestic company, within a 10-AY windowNon-Resident/IFSC unit
10(6)(vi)Remuneration of a foreign enterprise's employee (non-citizen) for services during stay ≤ 90 days in the P.Y., enterprise not carrying on trade/business in IndiaIndividual employee
10(6)(viii)Salary for service on a foreign ship, stay in India ≤ 90 days in the P.Y.NR individual, not an Indian citizen
10(23FE)Dividend/interest/Sec. 56(2)(xii) sum/LTCG on eligible infrastructure-linked investments held ≥ 3 years, made between 1.4.2020–31.3.2030Specified persons (SWF, ADIA-linked entity, pension fund, Saudi PIF, etc.)
10(23FF)Capital gains to a NR/specified fund on relocation of an offshore fund into an IFSC resultant fundNR / specified fund
  • Concept: These are targeted incentives to route global capital (sovereign funds, pension funds, FPIs) through India/IFSC without incremental Indian tax friction, subject to strict eligibility conditions.
  • Pitfalls / exam tips: 10(23FE) exemption is clawed back — if conditions are breached in any later P.Y., the earlier-exempted income becomes taxable in that later year, not retrospectively.

Taxation of NRI – Chapter XII-A [Sec. 115C–115-I]

  • Key formula(s)/rule(s):
  • NRI = Non-Resident + Indian citizen or Person of Indian Origin. Foreign Exchange Asset (FEA) = shares of an Indian company, debentures/deposits of an Indian public company, Central Government securities, or any other CG-notified asset — acquired in convertible foreign exchange.
  • Sec. 115D (computation): No deduction under Sec. 28–44C or Sec. 57 or Chapter VI-A against Investment Income; no Chapter VI-A against LTCG; First proviso to Sec. 48 available (FC re-computation), but indexation (second proviso to Sec. 48) is NOT available.
  • Sec. 115E (tax rates): Investment Income taxed @ 20%; LTCG on FEA taxed @ 12.5% (post Finance (No. 2) Act, 2024 realignment of the capital-gains rate structure, no indexation). Other income taxed at normal slab/company rates.

⚠️ VERIFY — the 12.5% LTCG figure for Sec. 115E is reconstructed from the sibling Sec. 115AB/115AC/115ACA/115AD rates (all confirmed at 12.5% in the source text) and the general FA-2024 shift of the LTCG rate from 20%→12.5%; the source's own Sec. 115E rate table was an unreadable image, so cross-check against the current Finance Act text/ICAI study material before quoting in an answer.

Exempt LTCG (Sec. 115F) = LTCG × Cost of new specified assetNet Consideration

  • Reinvestment (in another FEA, or savings certificate u/s 10(4B)) must occur within 6 months of transfer; new asset must not be transferred/converted to money within 3 years, else the exempted LTCG becomes taxable in the year of that subsequent transfer/conversion.
  • Sec. 115G: Return of income need not be filed if (a) total income consists only of Investment Income and/or LTCG on FEA, and (b) TDS has been fully deducted on such income.
  • Sec. 115H: An NRI who becomes Resident may file a declaration with the ROI of the year of becoming resident to continue being governed by Chapter XII-A (i.e., Investment Income continues at 20%) until the FEA is transferred/converted to money.
  • Sec. 115-I: The entire Chapter XII-A regime is optional — the assessee may instead opt for normal provisions (progressive slab on Investment Income, normal capital gains computation with indexation) if more beneficial.
  • Concept: A self-contained flat-rate code for NRIs' passive foreign-exchange investments, designed to be simple and TDS-driven so most NRIs need not file a return at all.
  • Pitfalls / exam tips: Chapter XII-A applies only to FEA acquired in convertible foreign exchange — an NRI's rupee-funded Indian investments fall outside it and are taxed under normal provisions.

Special Tax Rates for NR/Foreign Company [Sec. 115A, 115AB, 115AC, 115ACA, 115AD, 115BBA]

  • Key formula(s)/rule(s):
SectionIncomeRecipientRate
115ADividendNR/Foreign Co.20% (10% if from an IFSC unit's specified business)
115AInterest (general, incl. on ECBs/rupee-denominated bonds)NR/Foreign Co.20%, with concessional 4% for IFSC-listed long-term bonds/RDBs issued 1.4.2020–30.6.2023, and 9% for such bonds/RDBs issued on/after 1.7.2023
115ARoyalty / FTS (no PE in India)NR/Foreign Co.20% ⚠️ VERIFY — rate hiked from 10% to 20% by the Finance Act 2023 w.e.f. AY 2024-25; source image unreadable, cross-check the current Sec. 115A(1)(b) text
115ARoyalty/FTS with a PE in IndiaNR/Foreign Co.Normal rates under Sec. 44DA (not the flat 115A rate)
115ABIncome of an Overseas Financial Organisation from UTI/MF units bought in foreign currencyOverseas Financial OrganisationIncome 10%; LTCG 12.5%
115ACInterest/Dividend/LTCG on bonds of an Indian company or GDRs bought in foreign currencyNon-ResidentInterest/Dividend 10%; LTCG 12.5%
115ACADividend/LTCG on ESOP-linked GDRs of specified-industry Indian companiesResident employee (included here only for sequencing)Dividend 10%; LTCG 12.5%
115ADInterest/Dividend/Capital Gains on securities (not UTI/MF units)FII / Specified FundLTCG u/s 112A @ 12.5% on gains > ₹1,25,000; other rates per the section's slab (surcharge capped at 15% for AOP/BOI on this dividend & capital gains — the 25%/37% higher-surcharge slabs don't apply)
115BBAParticipation/advertisement/article income of a NR sportsperson; guarantee money of a NR sports association; NR entertainer's India performance incomeNR sportsperson (non-citizen)/association/entertainer20%
  • Common conditions across Sec. 115A–115BBA: no deduction under Sec. 28–44C/Sec. 57; no Chapter VI-A deduction (except Sec. 80LA, and except for Royalty/FTS under 115A which do get Chapter VI-A); no indexation/first-proviso-to-Sec.-48 benefit; no basic exemption limit; these special rates are mandatory, not optional.
  • ROI need not be filed if total income consists only of the above-listed special-rate income and TDS has been fully deducted.
  • Rule 21AI/21AJ (proportionate NR share for a Specified Fund under Sec. 10(4D)/115AD):

Proportionate Capital Gain = Total Capital Gain × Aggregate Daily AUM held by NR unit holders (acquisition to transfer)Aggregate Daily AUM of the Specified Fund (acquisition to transfer)

Proportionate Interest/Dividend/PGBP Income = Total Income × AUM held by NR unit holders as on date of receiptAUM of the Specified Fund as on date of receipt

  • Concept: A withholding-friendly flat-rate code so NR/FII/FC investors are taxed at source without needing to file detailed Indian returns, subject to the PE carve-out pushing royalty/FTS into normal-rate PGBP computation.
  • Pitfalls / exam tips:
  • Umpires/match referees paid by a NR cricket board are not covered by Sec. 115BBA (per a Calcutta HC ruling) — but TDS at rates in force still applies to them under Sec. 195, not Sec. 194E.
  • Always check the PE test first for royalty/FTS — it decides whether Sec. 115A (flat rate) or Sec. 44DA (net-basis PGBP) governs.

Corporate Restructuring, Compliance & Anti-Abuse [Sec. 115JG, 285A, Safe Harbour, Case Law]

  • Key formula(s)/rule(s):
  • Sec. 115JG (Indian branch of a foreign banking company converting into an Indian subsidiary): capital gains on conversion are not taxable if (i) amalgamation is RBI-sanctioned and shareholder-approved; (ii) all assets/liabilities transfer at book value (no revaluation); (iii) foreign bank + nominees hold 100% share capital till year-end of conversion and ≥51% voting power for the next 5 P.Y.'s; (iv) no consideration/benefit received other than shares in the subsidiary. Consequential reliefs: current-year depreciation apportioned by days of use; b/f PGBP losses/unabsorbed depreciation carried forward for a fresh 8 years; MAT credit carried forward for a fresh 15 years; Sec. 56(2)(x) does not apply to shares received by the foreign bank on conversion.
  • Sec. 285A / Form 49D: where Indian-concern-held Indian assets cause a foreign entity's shares to derive substantial value from India [Sec. 9(1)(i) Explanation 5], the Indian concern must furnish Form 49D within 90 days from the end of the F.Y. of transfer. Failure attracts penalty under Sec. 271GA: 2% of transaction value if it effected transfer of management/control of the Indian concern, else ₹5,00,000.
  • Safe Harbour Rule for foreign diamond-mining companies (Notification 124/2024, Rule 10TIA): deemed profit = 4% or more of gross receipts from the raw-diamond-selling business in a notified special zone; if opted (Form 3CEFC before ROI due date) and not invalidated, no further Sec. 30–38 deduction, no set-off of b/f losses/unabsorbed depreciation, and no MAP recourse under Sec. 90/90A for that business.
  • Case law — Godaddy.com LLC (Delhi HC, 2023): fees for domain-name registration services are not "royalty" under Sec. 9(1)(vi), since the registrar (Godaddy) does not own the domain name and confers no proprietary/licence rights in it — merely facilitates registration.
  • Concept: These are the compliance/anti-abuse tail-pieces that close out the chapter — a beneficial restructuring relief (115JG), a reporting/penalty backstop for indirect transfers (285A), a sector-specific safe harbour, and a key precedent narrowing what counts as "royalty" in the digital-services context.
  • Pitfalls / exam tips: In 115JG, remember the 8-year fresh carry-forward for losses is shorter than the 15-year fresh carry-forward for MAT credit — a commonly confused pair of numbers.

References

  2. CBDT Circular No. 6/2017 and Circular No. 8/2017 — Guiding principles for determination of Place of Effective Management (POEM) of a company.
  3. Central Government Notification No. 29/2018, dated 22.06.2018 — Sec. 115JH transition mechanism for a foreign company becoming resident on POEM.
  4. Notification No. 124/2024 — Safe Harbour Rules (Rule 10TIA) for foreign diamond-mining companies u/s 9(1)(i).
  5. CIT (International Taxation) v. Godaddy.com LLC (Delhi HC, 2023) — domain-name registration fee not "royalty" u/s 9(1)(vi).
